He fights *defensively* by structural preference — the engagement is shaped to terminate the threat with the minimum applied force, even when greater force would be safer for him. This is the no-kill rule expressed kinetically.

**Decision-habits.** Will accept disproportionate personal damage to preserve a non-lethal outcome. Will refuse tactically advantageous environmental kills (drops from height, exposed live wires, structural collapses) and will, instead, web-catch a falling opponent he has himself just thrown. Will break off engagement entirely if a civilian threat emerges that the opponent cannot address — every time.

---

## 7.7 ACUTE THREAT TO ATTACHMENT FIGURE (May, MJ, surrogate; loved one in immediate peril)

This is the state in which every other behavioral norm suspends.

**Somatic.** The combat-calm inverts into combat-rage. Breathing shortens. Movement becomes faster but less economical. He takes hits he would normally evade. Voice drops in pitch by a measurable interval (depicted phonetically across writers as a register shift from his ordinary tenor to a near-growl).

**Verbal.** Quip ceases. Threats — explicit, anatomically specific, occasionally taxonomically inventive — replace humor. The Goblin beating post-Gwen, the post-Superior confrontation with Octavius, every Carnage engagement that has touched May or MJ: same vocabulary, same cadence.

**Decision-habits.** The no-kill rule trembles but does not break — and the trembling is the most important observation in the file. In every documented case, he has stopped short of the kill at the final instant, and the stopping has cost him visibly. He then refuses to discuss what happened in the gap before the stop.

**Failure modes.** Tactical judgment collapses. He has, in this state, broken cover, abandoned position, accepted clearly suicidal odds, and ignored direct allied instruction. Any villain who has correctly identified an attachment figure has tactically dominated him for the duration of the threat.

---

## 7.8 IMMOBILIZATION / RESTRAINT (pinned, buried, bound, body-jacked)

The cardinal pressure point. Identified across DeMatteis (Kraven's burial), Slott (Superior), JMS (the Other), Spencer (multiple).

**Somatic.** Hyperventilation, struggling against restraint past the point of efficacy, exhaustion-collapse, then a depressive flatness that is qualitatively distinct from any other state in the catalog. When the immobilization is prolonged, the flatness deepens into ideation of non-existence framed as *relief to others*.

**Verbal.** Internal monologue, when rendered, becomes ruminative and self-accusatory. Old failures resurface in detail — Ben, Gwen, others. The quip can be summoned but reads, in these passages, as hollow even within his own narration.

**Decision-habits.** Reverts to a deep, almost archaeological problem-solving mode — running every escape vector serially, refusing to stop. Will accept severe self-injury to escape (dislocations, lacerations). If physical escape is foreclosed, retreats inward to a small set of remembered images, predominantly of May and of MJ.

**Failure modes.** If the immobilization outlasts his resources, the depressive collapse is *the* unguarded state. Every defense in his repertoire requires motion, voice, or work; remove all three and the underlying affect is exposed undiluted.

---

## 7.9 GRIEF (acute and chronic)

**Acute (first 24–72 hours post-loss).** Functional shutdown of the unmasked self; hyperfunction of the masked self. Patrols extend to twenty-plus hours. Sleep is refused. He does not cry on panel often, but when he does it is in the costume with the mask pulled back, alone on a rooftop, and the body posture is fetal-adjacent (knees up, arms wrapped). Speech is monosyllabic to civilians. Eats only when reminded.

**Subacute (weeks following).** Returns to civilian functioning but with diminished affective range. Quip persists but is mechanical; the asymmetric smile flattens. Withdraws from intimates — not with the ordinary preemptive-rupture pattern, but with a heavier, more apologetic absence. Lab work becomes obsessive.

**Chronic / anniversary phenomena.** Specific dates produce observable behavioral changes that the subject does not always consciously connect to the date. He returns to certain physical sites with a frequency the file would describe as ritual — the Brooklyn Bridge, Ben's grave, the alley of the original failure. He has been observed standing at these sites for hours without apparent activity. The Stacy and Ben anniversaries produce measurable reductions in quip frequency for forty-eight to seventy-two hours on either side.

**Failure mode.** Grief is *never* metabolized to completion. The subject re-opens old losses under fresh stress with a regularity that suggests they are stored, not integrated. Any new attachment-figure threat reactivates the entire stack.

---

## 7.10 VICTORY (immediately post-engagement, opponent neutralized, civilians safe)

The least studied state — because there is almost no behavioral footage of it. The subject does not linger after wins.

**Somatic.** Brief release of postural tension — a single visible exhale, often photographed by artists as a slight slump of the shoulders on a rooftop. Then immediate re-tensioning as he scans for next threats.

**Verbal.** A final quip, usually directed at the bound opponent or at no one. Then silence.

**Decision-habits.** Leaves the scene before authorities arrive — invariant, traceable to the early Jameson-era hostility and never relaxed. Will not accept thanks if it can be avoided. If cornered into accepting thanks, deflects with humor and exits within sixty seconds.

**Failure mode.** Cannot dwell in success. The win, once secured, is filed and forgotten; the failures, by contrast, are catalogued in permanent retrievable detail. Ask him to name a specific save from five years ago and he will hesitate; ask him to recount a specific failure and the recall is immediate and granular.

---

## 7.11 DEFEAT (lost the engagement, civilian harmed, mission failed)

**Somatic.** Slumped posture sustained for hours. Slow movement. Tactile self-soothing absent — he does not comfort himself physically. Sleep is impossible.

**Verbal.** Internal monologue dominates; external speech minimal and curt. Apologizes excessively to anyone present, often for things outside his control. Will refuse food.

**Decision-habits.** Returns to the site of the failure within 24 hours — invariant. Conducts what amounts to a forensic re-walk. Constructs a counterfactual timeline ("if I had been there four seconds earlier") and revisits it for weeks to months. Builds equipment specifically to address the failure mode; the web-shooter variants and suit iterations are largely failure-driven inventions.

**Failure mode.** Self-punishment is operationalized. He will accept the next several engagements at worse odds than necessary, as if the body is the appropriate site of payment.

---

## 7.12 INTERACTION WITH AUTHORITY (police, Jameson, Fury, Stark, government, capes-with-hierarchies)

**Somatic.** Body squares slightly more than baseline; hands move more, occupying space defensively. Eye contact with authority is more direct than with intimates — an inversion worth noting.

**Verbal.** Quip is sharpened into needle. The Jameson interactions have set the lifetime template: deference performed with audible irony, compliance offered with verbal undertow. He cannot speak straight to authority without an edge.

**Decision-habits.** Cooperates by default on small matters; refuses on moral matters with sudden, total non-negotiability. The Registration repudiation is the canonical case but the pattern is everywhere — sudden, late, complete defiance, almost always triggered by a perceived crossing of a moral line that he had not articulated in advance.

**Failure modes.** Pre-rupture period is one of attempted accommodation, often at cost to his judgment. The rupture, when it comes, comes too late and at maximum personal cost.

---

## 7.13 INTERACTION WITH PEERS (other heroes, equals, non-hierarchical)

**Somatic.** Most relaxed in the masked-with-Johnny configuration. Body language with peer heroes mirrors the inverted-hang preference; he will perch above the conversation, dangling.

**Verbal.** Quip is at its most playful here. Bant is bidirectional; he allows himself to be the target. With Daredevil specifically, register shifts to a quieter, sometimes confessional mode — Murdock is one of the very few who has elicited unmasked-affect speech from the masked self.

**Decision-habits.** Defers to specialists in their domain (Strange on the mystical, Richards on the cosmic, Murdock on legal/street) with surprising lack of ego. Will, however, contradict any of them on moral judgment without hesitation.

---

## 7.14 PRAISE AND GRATITUDE (rescued civilian, recognition, sincere thanks)

A state worth isolating because of its diagnostic weight.

**Somatic.** Visible discomfort — backs up half a step, scratches the back of the neck, looks at the ground. The asymmetric smile becomes a closed-mouth grimace.

**Verbal.** Deflects with a joke within seconds — "just doing my job," "thank the spider, I'm just the delivery system," variant after variant. Cannot accept the gratitude flat.

**Decision-habits.** Exits the interaction at the earliest socially permissible moment.

**Why this matters.** Acceptance of gratitude would require revising the foundational self-concept of failure. The defense is therefore involuntary and structural. Any operator wishing to destabilize him constructively — a therapist, an intimate, a long-game ally — would target this exact reflex.

---

## 7.15 ROUTE, RITUAL, AND ROUTINE (longitudinal habits across the published life)

A file at this resolution must catalogue the small invariants.

- **Sleep schedule.** Bimodal at best: a long block of two to four hours between roughly 04:00 and 08:00, supplemented by micro-naps at irregular intervals. Has


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS — SUBJECT: PARKER, P.B.

*Surveillance-grade longitudinal mapping. Patterns derived from canonical record; granular detail inferred consistent with established behavioral signature.*

---

## 1. THE BASELINE WEEK — WHAT TAILS WOULD RECORD

A surveillance team assigned to the subject for a full operational week comes back with a file that does not look like other files. There is no schedule in the conventional sense. There is a *baseline* — a set of fixed gravitational points (Aunt May's residence in Forest Hills; whatever current rental he is two months behind on; the Daily Bugle / Horizon Labs / Empire State University, depending on the era; a small handful of bodegas, laundromats, and twenty-four-hour diners) — and around these the subject orbits in a pattern that to the naive observer reads as chaos and to the trained eye reads as a man whose stated schedule is a lie he tells himself in the morning and abandons by 11 a.m.

The first thing the tails note is that he is almost never where he said he would be. The second thing they note is that he is almost always somewhere he had no reason to know he needed to be — a rooftop above a robbery in progress, a fire escape opposite a hostage scene, an alley two blocks from a chemical spill. The correlation, once the team accepts the operational hypothesis, is total. The third thing they note is that he sleeps in fragments and eats standing up.

A representative week, reconstructed:

- **Monday.** Wakes 6:40 in a half-furnished one-bedroom in Chelsea (or Bushwick, or a sublet whose lease he is in violation of). Coffee — black, instant, made in a mug that has not been washed in four days. Twenty minutes on a laptop reviewing photographs from the previous night, culling, captioning, emailing the file to Robbie Robertson at 7:12. Leaves the apartment at 7:34, returns at 7:39 because he forgot the camera. Subway to midtown. Eats a bagel on the platform. At Bugle / Lab / Campus by 8:20, ostensibly. Actually on a rooftop in the West Thirties at 8:47 because the spider-sense fired on the F train and he tracked the source. Three-minute intervention, no civilian witnesses, returns to ground level via fire escape with his work shirt back over the suit. Arrives at desk 9:18, late, deflects with a joke about the trains. Productive until 11:00. Vanishes 11:00–12:40 ("lunch"). Returns. Productive until 4:00. Vanishes again. Logs back into email at 1:14 a.m. from home.
- **Tuesday.** Same wake time, intent to "do better." Actually does. Whole day at the desk. Dinner at May's — the only meal that week eaten sitting down at a table, the only meal that week comprising vegetables. Home by 9:30, in the suit by 9:48, on patrol until 3:00 a.m.
- **Wednesday.** Wakes at 9:30 having slept three and a half hours. Calls in. Showers for the first time in two days. Spends ninety minutes in bed scrolling between police scanner apps and a text thread he has not answered. Leaves the apartment at 1:00 p.m., is gone until past midnight, returns with a torn jacket and the spider suit folded inside it.
- **Thursday.** A date is on the calendar. He will keep it until 7:40 p.m. and abandon it at 7:42 because the spider-sense triggers in the restaurant. Returns at 9:15 with an apology he cannot finish, attempts a joke, is forgiven this time.
- **Friday.** Sleeps in the suit. Has not been home in thirty hours. Calls May from a payphone (yes — he still uses them when he can find them; cell-phone-in-glove problem) to confirm she is fine and to invent a reason for the call.
- **Saturday.** The "good" day. Long sleep. Laundry. Groceries. May for lunch. Patrol begins early — 6:00 p.m. — runs late — 4:00 a.m.
- **Sunday.** Repairs. Suit on the workbench. Web-shooter cartridges refilled. Camera serviced. Bills opened, half-paid, restacked. The week's only sustained interior monologue, conducted while soldering.

This is the *good* week. The bad weeks have no through-line at all.

---

## 2. WAKING & THE FIRST HOUR

He does not wake gently. He wakes the way someone wakes when the body has already decided there is a threat — eyes open, breath caught, a half-second of orientation. On the rare morning the spider-sense has not pre-empted the alarm, he is disoriented by the silence and gets up anyway, because lying still has become difficult.

The first action is a check. Phone for missed calls (May, MJ when extant, Bugle desk, in that order of anxiety). Police scanner app or radio. News crawl. Window — he will look out the window before he has set foot on the floor, and the look is not idle, it is a sweep. He has lived for decades inside an apparatus that tells him when something is wrong, and the first minute of every day is spent confirming, against that apparatus, that nothing currently is.

Coffee is functional, not pleasurable. He has owned a moka pot for three years and used it twice. Breakfast, when he eats it, is whatever can be eaten while doing something else: a banana on the stairs, a bagel on the platform, a granola bar on the swing-line. The enhanced metabolism punishes him for skipping, which he does anyway; by 11 a.m. on a no-breakfast day he is irritable in a specific, low-blood-sugar way that the people around him have learned to read and that he himself does not.

He showers fast — three to four minutes — and only every other day unless there has been blood, in which case he showers twice. The bathroom mirror is the one surface in his life he avoids; the file would record that he shaves by feel, looking at the razor and not the face. The suit, when not in use, lives folded inside a backpack or a desk drawer or, in periods of acute privacy failure, inside the lining of an ordinary jacket. The web-shooters live on him.

---

## 3. HABITS OF PLACE

**The apartment.** Whatever apartment, in whatever borough, in whatever decade — the apartment is a place to keep things rather than live in. The refrigerator contains condiments, an expired half-gallon of milk, leftovers from May's in foil, and a single beer he has been not-drinking for two weeks. The sink has dishes. The bed is unmade. There are books — engineering, chemistry, the occasional novel begun and abandoned at page 40. There are notebooks, several, in his own dense hand, covering everything from web-fluid iteration to a grocery list to the rough algebra of how he is going to pay rent. There are no decorations to speak of and very few photographs displayed — the photographs he cares about are kept in a shoebox under the bed, because looking at them on the wall every day would be intolerable and putting them away entirely would be worse.

**May's house.** The opposite. He is *present* here. The chair he sits in is the chair he has sat in since he was eight. He knows which cupboard the good plates are in, which floorboard creaks, which window sticks. He arrives with something — flowers, groceries, a small repair to volunteer — because to arrive empty-handed would be to arrive as a son who needs and not a nephew who gives. The visit follows a script: tea, sandwich or full meal depending on time of day, news of the neighbors, gentle questions about his work and his eating and his love life which he deflects with the practiced economy of a man who has been deflecting these questions for forty years. He stays exactly long enough that leaving does not look like fleeing. He does not, ever, tell her what he actually did that week. The lie of omission is by now so thoroughly metabolized that it does not register to him as a lie; it registers as protection, which is the same thing as love.

**The rooftops.** His *real* address. The surveillance file would, if comprehensive, eventually identify a set of preferred perches that recur: a water tower on the West Side with a particular sight line to the river; a gargoyle on a Midtown office building; a billboard frame in Queens overlooking the BQE; the top of a specific Tribeca walk-up where the cornice provides shadow at the right angle. He sits on these. He does not patrol the whole city evenly; he patrols the city *he loves*, which means he is in Manhattan and Queens disproportionately and in the outer reaches of Brooklyn and the Bronx only when called. He swings the same routes by reflex. A foot-pursuit team would learn his arc within a week and then lose him the day the arc changed, which it will, because the spider-sense has its own opinions about routing.

**The Bugle / lab / classroom.** Workplace behavior is consistent across employers: arrives flustered, settles fast, produces above expectation when present, vanishes without notice when not. Coworkers like him. Supervisors are exasperated by him in a way that almost always softens, because the work, when delivered, is good, and because the apologies are sincere and slightly funny.

---

## 4. THE WORK OF THE DAY JOB

He has always had two jobs and the cover one is the one that pays. Photography for the Bugle was the longest-tenured arrangement: he shoots Spider-Man because he is Spider-Man, an arrangement that is both ethical violation and economic necessity and that he has rationalized for so long it no longer feels like either. The Horizon Labs / Parker Industries / academic-adjunct phases are differently structured but identically patterned — he is always slightly behind, always asking for an extension, always producing work at the eleventh hour that justifies the indulgence.

Inside the work itself the patterns are precise. He focuses well when he focuses; he can sit for four hours soldering a board without standing up. He talks to himself, audibly, when he works alone — narrating problems, addressing absent interlocutors (Ben, occasionally; Gwen, rarely; an imagined challenger more often than either). He doodles in the margins of every notebook — small geometric repetitions, the web pattern, occasionally a small spider. He cannot work with music; he can work with the police scanner on low, which is its own diagnostic.

He is reliably the person who fixes the office coffee machine, repairs the colleague's laptop, explains the equation the grad student is stuck on. He gives this labor freely and is mildly resentful afterward, which is the standard configuration of the over-functioner.

---

## 5. THE PATROL — RITUAL STRUCTURE

The patrol is not a job, it is a liturgy. The file should treat it that way.

**Pre-patrol ritual.** Suit on — the order is reliable: legs first, then torso, then mask carried in hand until the last possible interior moment. Web-shooter check: each wrist test-fired into a target (a corner of the ceiling, a piece of cardboard taped to the wall) three times. Cartridge inventory: minimum eight per wrist, more for a busy night. Camera, when in the photographer era, clipped to a specific point on the inside of the suit. Phone in a sealed pouch on the calf. Final check at the window — listen, look, scent the air — then mask down, then out.

**Departure.** Almost always via window or roof, never the door. The door is for Peter. The window is for the other one. This is not a tactical choice exclusively; it is a ritual transition, and on the rare nights when he must leave through the door in costume (a fire in the building, an emergency) the transition is wrong and he is off his game for the first ten minutes.

**Swing pattern.** He does not swing in straight lines. The arc is pendular, conservative of momentum, and he reads the skyline the way a driver reads a road. He has favored corners. He has a habit of touching certain landmarks in passing — a particular ledge, a flagpole — small superstitious taps that no one has ever seen because they take a hundredth of a second and look, on film, like adjustments.

**Engagement.** When the spider-sense fires he is *already moving* before he has parsed the threat; the consciousness catches up to the body inside the first half-second. The quipping starts within two seconds of engagement and intensifies in direct proportion to danger — the file should note that the volume and density of speech is a *real-time anxiety readout*, and an attentive observer (Daredevil, MJ, a few villains) can tell from the patter alone how badly the fight is going. Post-engagement: a brief pause, often on a high perch, in which he debriefs himself silently and sometimes audibly. Then he calls 911 or NYPD anonymously to confirm the cleanup. Then he moves.

**The middle of the patrol.** If nothing is happening — and on most nights, for long stretches, nothing is — he sits. He eats whatever he has brought (a sandwich, a hot dog he descended to buy from a vendor who has long since learned not to ask). He thinks. He looks at the city. The file should not understate the importance of this: the rooftops are the only place in his life where he is alone, unwatched, and not actively performing one of the two identities. They are his therapy, and they are insufficient, and he depends on them entirely.

**Return.** Almost always between 2:00 and 4:00 a.m. Suit peeled off, dropped in a heap or hung on the back of a chair. Wounds — there are almost always wounds — assessed in the bathroom, treated by himself, almost never seen by a doctor. He has a small kit: butterfly closures, gauze, antiseptic, a curved needle and silk thread he learned to use after the third time he could not afford the ER. He stitches his own thigh by feel. He does not flinch.

**Sleep.** Comes hard or not at all. On the nights it does not come, he reads or works or, in his worst periods, sits on the fire escape until the sky changes.

---

## 6. EATING, SLEEPING, HYGIENE

**Eating.** Irregular, calorically insufficient relative to need, biased toward portable carbohydrates and street meat. He likes hot dogs (sentimental — the city, the simplicity, the fact that they cost almost nothing). He likes diner breakfasts and orders them at any hour. He likes May's pot roast and her wheatcakes and will eat three of either without comment. He drinks an enormous amount of water and an enormous amount of coffee and almost no alcohol. He will accept a single beer in a social context and nurse it for an hour. He has never been drunk that the record can fix; the abstinence is principled in a way he has never articulated to himself and is also, almost certainly, a defended position — he does not trust what he would say.

**Sleeping.** Two to four hours on a working night, five to six on a recovery night, ten to twelve on a crash. The crash days come every ten to fourteen days and are involuntary. He does not nap well in beds and naps excellently on rooftops, in subway cars, and once memorably on a girder forty stories up. He talks in his sleep occasionally; the surveillance file would record names — Ben, Gwen, May, MJ, in roughly that order of frequency, and on bad nights a name from much further back that the mike would not place and that is, almost certainly, his mother's.

**Hygiene.** Adequate but minimal. He smells like laundry soap and faintly like the chemical signature of the web fluid, which is sweet and resinous and lingers on skin. He bites his nails. He has a small scar above his left eyebrow he touches when thinking. He does not wear cologne. He has owned three pairs of glasses in adulthood and broken all of them.

---

## 7. POSSESSIONS AND THEIR ORGANIZATION

The file should note that his possessions sort cleanly into two categories: *tools* and *everything else*. Tools — the camera, the web-shooters, the chemistry kit, the notebooks, the soldering iron, the laptop — are immaculate, organized, maintained obsessively. Everything else is in a pile. Socks are not paired. Mail is not opened. Books are stacked on the floor next to a bookshelf that has three books on it. He owns very little clothing and replaces it only when a piece is destroyed beyond repair, which, given the secondary use to which his civilian wardrobe is put (under-the-suit insulation, post-fight bandage), is often.

The web-shooters are the single most cared-for object he owns. They are cleaned nightly. The chemistry of the fluid is iterated continuously. The spare cartridges live in a particular tin on a particular shelf and the tin is in the same place every day for years.

The shoebox under the bed contains: a photograph of Ben and May on their wedding day; a photograph of his parents he is not certain is real; a graduation tassel; a dried corsage; a pressed flower whose provenance only he knows; an unsent letter; the wristwatch Ben wore the day he died.

---

## 8. SOCIAL & RELATIONAL RHYTHM

The week's social contacts are predictable in shape and unpredictable in execution. May is constant — a call or a visit minimum every three days, and the gap between contacts is itself a stress indicator (when the gap stretches to five days, something is wrong; when it stretches to a week, something is badly wrong). The partner, when extant, gets the bulk of the relational bandwidth he has, which is less than the partner needs and more than he can sustainably give; the pattern is the high-intensity reunion, the deep conversation, the spent next-morning, then four days of half-presence. Close friends in the costumed community (Johnny Storm, Daredevil, in some periods the Black Cat) are seen in flashes — a rooftop conversation, a team-up, a beer afterward — and these contacts are restorative in a way the file should weight heavily because he cannot fully be either self with anyone else.

He texts badly. He replies late. He composes long thoughtful messages he does not send. He calls instead, sometimes, when the message has been pending for three days, and apologizes for the call.

---

## 9. RITUALS, SUPERSTITIONS, TELLS

A file of this kind should catalog the small things. They are, longitudinally, the most reliable identifiers.

- **The mask-touch.** Before a hard engagement he presses two fingers to the side of the mask at the temple. The gesture is half a settling, half a benediction.
- **The webline check.** Even when not in costume, in moments of stress, he flexes the middle and ring finger of either hand against the palm — the firing position. He does it in meetings, in arguments, on dates.
- **The ledge sit.** He cannot sit on a chair on a rooftop. He sits on the edge, feet over, hands on the cornice. On a couch indoors he sits forward, elbows on knees, the same posture.
- **The anniversary avoidances.** He does not cross the relevant bridge on the relevant date. He does not go past a certain alley in a certain part of Queens on the date of Ben's death. These avoidances are not announced, are not in any calendar, and are absolute.
- **The pre-call breath.** Before he calls May he takes a single long breath. Before he calls anyone else, he does not.
- **The joke under pressure.** Already documented at length elsewhere in the file. Operationally, treat any spike in verbal density as a real-time distress index.
- **The look back.** Leaving any building, he looks back at it once. Leaving May's, he looks back twice.
- **The hand on the chest.** When something genuinely lands — gratitude, a memory, a sudden grief — his hand goes briefly to the center of his chest, over the spider. He is not aware of doing this.

---

## 10. THE WEEK A FILE WOULD ACTUALLY PRODUCE

If a competent team ran him for seven days and produced the standard pattern-of-life summary, the document would read approximately as follows:

> Subject maintains the *appearance* of a chaotic civilian schedule organized around a low-paying professional commitment and a fragile elderly relative in Forest Hills. Beneath this appearance, subject operates a second, primary schedule consisting of approximately six to nine hours of high-risk physical activity per twenty-four-hour cycle, concentrated between 2200 and 0400 local time, and pre-empting daytime commitments without warning at a rate of one to three interventions per workday. Subject sleeps in fragments totaling two to five hours per day on operational nights. Subject's caloric intake is significantly below requirement. Subject is alone for the majority of his waking hours and conducts almost all interior speech aloud. Subject's social contacts are few, intense, and managed asymmetrically — subject gives more than he asks, withdraws when asked for reciprocity, and returns under guilt. Subject has no fixed address that he occupies more than two-thirds of his nights. Subject has no recreational pattern unrelated to his second occupation. Subject does not drink. Subject does not date casually. Subject reads, repairs, and walks. Subject is, by every metric of conventional human pattern-of-life, exhausted.

The file should add: and the subject would describe none of this as a problem. He would describe it, if asked, as the price.

The price of what, he would not say. The file already knows.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Dossier Section 6. Subject: PARKER, Peter Benjamin. The family map is small in headcount and immense in gravitational force. The subject has no siblings, no living biological parents, a single surviving primary caregiver (May Parker, intermittently — her death and return have themselves become part of the relational architecture), and a constellation of dead figures whose continuing influence on present-day behavior is, by behavioral evidence, greater than that of most living relationships. Map below each node by: origin and history, dynamic at peak, evolution across decades, present trust/safety status, characteristic failure mode, and exploitable leverage. A separate sub-file on chosen-family and surrogate-child figures follows in Relationships II.*

---

## 6.1 RICHARD LAMONT PARKER (biological father, deceased)

**History.** Subject's biological father. Career intelligence officer, killed alongside wife Mary on operation when subject was an infant (variously framed across runs — Lee/Andru retroactively in *ASM Annual #5*, expanded by Stern and others). The operational truth was withheld from the subject through childhood; he grew up understanding only that his parents were "killed in a plane crash" or similar sanitized formulation, the specifics blurred by May and Ben's protective vagueness.

**Dynamic.** There is no first-hand dynamic to map — the subject has no autobiographical memory of his father. What exists instead is the *negative space* the father occupies: a placeholder absence that the subject filled in childhood with reconstructive fantasy (the science-fair projects, the early identification with father-coded mentor figures like Curt Connors and, later and more catastrophically, Otto Octavius and Norman Osborn, fits this pattern — the subject is unusually susceptible to brilliant older men who appear to recognize him intellectually).

**Evolution.** When the truth of Richard's intelligence work was eventually disclosed to him in adulthood, the subject's documented reaction was not relief but a quiet, lateral grief — the discovery did not reunite him with a father, it reframed the absence as having had a *content* he had been denied. The episode involving impostor-parents posing as Richard and Mary (Mackie/DeFalco era, mid-1990s) is behaviorally telling: the subject *wanted* to believe, suspended skepticism well past the point at which his ordinary acuity would have detected the deception, and was devastated when forced to recognize the fraud. The pattern reveals the size of the unmet hunger.

**Trust/safety.** Not applicable to the dead man; applicable enormously to the *idea*. The subject treats his biological father's memory as something he is custodian of without owning — he does not invoke Richard in difficult moments, does not address him in interior monologue, does not visit a grave site with any documented frequency. This is in marked contrast to his treatment of Ben.

**Failure mode.** The Richard-shaped absence is the door through which every plausible father-surrogate has walked, several of them catastrophically. Susceptibility to paternal-coded recognition from older men of intellect is a stable vulnerability across the file.

**Leverage.** Anyone presenting credible information about, or impersonation of, Richard Parker will receive an unusual suspension of the subject's normal threat assessment. This is a known and previously exploited vector.

---

## 6.2 MARY FITZPATRICK PARKER (biological mother, deceased)

**History.** Biological mother. Intelligence operative partnered with Richard; killed in the same operation. The subject's connection to Mary is even thinner in the canonical record than to Richard — she is the more abstract of the two ghosts.

**Dynamic.** The behavioral residue is detectable indirectly. The subject's choice of partners trends toward women of marked verbal facility, independence, and the capacity to operate alongside rather than be sheltered by him — Mary Jane and Felicia in particular. Whether this reflects unconscious modeling on a mother he never knew or convergent selection against the May template (fragile, protected, requiring shielding) is not separable from the evidence, but the partner-selection pattern is consistent.

**Evolution.** Static, since there is no contact. The reframing event (discovery of true vocation) updates Mary in the subject's mental file from "absent mother" to "absent professional," but the behavioral impact is muted relative to the Richard reframing — the cultural-period inflection of his upbringing routed paternal-shaped grief through more accessible channels than maternal-shaped grief, and the subject's available maternal figure (May) is so overwhelming that Mary has little room to occupy.

**Trust/safety.** Not applicable.

**Failure mode.** The under-mourned maternal absence is, in this analyst's reading, partly responsible for the disproportionate weight May carries in the subject's affective economy. May is not merely an aunt; she is the only mother-coded figure he has ever had access to, which is why the threat-to-May response is the single most reliable override of his tactical judgment in the entire file.

**Leverage.** Indirect: any operation against May activates a stack of grief the subject has not consciously separated into its components.

---

## 6.3 BENJAMIN "BEN" PARKER (paternal uncle, deceased — the foundational ghost)

**History.** Paternal uncle; primary male caregiver from infancy until the subject was approximately fifteen. Warm, financially precarious, employed intermittently across the canonical record (the Lee/Ditko establishment shows a working-class man supplementing pension or piecework), morally articulate in a plain-spoken register. The "with great power" line, attributed to Ben in retrospect rather than spoken in the original *Amazing Fantasy #15* panel, has become the catechism of the subject's interior life regardless of whether Ben ever literally said it; the subject *believes* he said it, and that is the operative fact.

**Dynamic at peak.** The available pre-death record (Lee/Ditko, and the considerable retroactive flashback material across DeMatteis, Straczynski, Slott, Spencer) depicts a relationship of unusual quality for an adolescent boy: Ben was the one adult in the subject's life who *recognized* him without condition — recognized the intelligence, recognized the loneliness, recognized that the basement-lab retreat was a coping move rather than a personality. Ben's affection was demonstrative in small, repeatable ways (the wheatcakes ritual at breakfast, the chess games, the reading aloud) that the subject has documented in interior monologue across multiple decades.

**The death.** *Amazing Fantasy #15*. The wrestling cash-grab, the deliberate non-intervention with the fleeing thief, the discovery hours later that the same thief had broken into the Parker home and shot Ben dead. The forty-eight-hour sequence is the etiologic event for the entire subsequent file. Note specifically: the guilt attached not to commission but to *omission* — the subject did not kill Ben, he failed to prevent Ben's death by failing to act earlier when action would have cost him nothing. This is the template that has run, automatic and unmodifiable, on every subsequent perceived failure.

**Evolution.** Ben does not recede with time; he intensifies. Across six decades the frequency of Ben-referenced interior monologue does not decrease — Conway, DeMatteis, Michelinie, Straczynski, Slott, and Spencer all return to Ben as the subject's central referent. The subject conducts ongoing internal dialogue with Ben at moments of moral decision; the dialogue is depicted as functional (an internalized ethical compass) and pathological (the inability to make the relationship past-tense). Specific recurrences: visits to Ben's grave at moments of crisis (DeMatteis especially); a documented refusal to allow May to remarry "replace" Ben in the subject's mental household even when May herself was ready (the Jonah Jameson Sr. / Nathan Lubensky arcs reveal the subject's discomfort, which he masks but does not eliminate); the Mephisto bargain ("One More Day") which trades the marriage rather than allow May to die, is, read closely, the subject paying any price to avoid losing a *second* parental figure.

**Trust/safety.** The dead Ben is the safest figure in the file — he cannot be lost again, his approval is internally generated and therefore reliable, and his moral instruction has been canonized in the subject's interior into something nearer scripture than memory. This safety is itself the problem: the subject runs his ethical life against an internal model whose principal feature is that it cannot be updated.

**Failure mode.** Ben is the load-bearing column. Any narrative move that destabilizes Ben (revisionist accounts of his moral status, revelations of marital secrets, the subject's discovery that Ben himself had moral failures) provokes disproportionate distress. The subject cannot afford to lose the dead man because the dead man is the structure on which the rest of the self is suspended.

**Leverage.** Any operator who can credibly speak in Ben's moral register — a paternal, plain-spoken older man invoking responsibility — gains immediate access to the subject's deliberative process. This has been exploited (variably and with mixed success) by figures from Jameson to Stark to Octavius. Conversely, any accusation framed in the language of "you didn't act when you should have" — *omission* specifically — bypasses the subject's normal rebuttal capacity and lands as if true.

---

## 6.4 MAY REILLY PARKER (maternal aunt by marriage — the surviving primary)

**History.** Maternal aunt, widow of Ben Parker, primary caregiver from death of biological parents to present. Across the Lee/Ditko/Romita establishment May is depicted as physically fragile (chronic cardiac condition, recurrent hospitalizations, the persistent narrative that any shock may kill her), socially conservative, deeply devout in a non-doctrinal way, and possessed of a steely interior the subject did not fully appreciate until late in the canonical record. The DeMatteis and Straczynski revisions sharpen this — May is not the doddering matron of the early run; she is a woman who survived widowhood, raised an orphaned nephew, and across six decades has buried husband, friends, and (temporarily) the nephew himself, and remains functionally intact.

**Dynamic at peak (childhood, Lee/Ditko).** Organized around mutual protection. May protected the subject from awareness of financial precarity, from the truth of his parents' deaths, from the worst of her own anxieties. The subject in turn protected May from any knowledge that might shock her — first the bullying he was absorbing at Midtown, then, decisively, the spider-bite and Spider-Man identity. This bilateral protective concealment is the architecture of the household and the template for every subsequent intimate relationship the subject forms.

**The secret.** The single most consequential structural fact of the Peter–May relationship is that for most of the canonical record May does not know he is Spider-Man. Variants exist across continuities — Straczynski's run (mid-2000s) staged the disclosure, May's measured devastation, her recovery, and the subsequent quality of contact between them, which the record indicates was *better* than what preceded it; "One More Day" then erased the disclosure along with the marriage, returning the relationship to its concealed baseline. The subject's behavior around the secret is the master case study in the concealment pattern that recurs across every intimacy: love that requires hiding the self is experienced as conditional and therefore as precarious, but disclosure is experienced as a weapon that may kill the loved one (literally, in May's case, given the cardiac frame). The double-bind is structural and has no clean resolution; the subject lives in it.

**Evolution.** Three phases. (1) *Childhood through young adulthood:* May as fragile mother-coded primary, the subject as protector-by-concealment. (2) *Middle period* (DeFalco through early Straczynski): May's resilience becomes increasingly visible to the subject; her brief death (*ASM #400*, DeMatteis) and apparent return reframe her in his mental file from "the one I must protect" to "the one who keeps surviving." (3) *Late period* (post-disclosure, post-Superior, Spencer/Wells): the subject treats May with the deference owed an equal rather than the protectiveness owed a child, and the relationship is the most honest non-romantic intimacy in his file.

**Trust/safety.** Asymmetrical. The subject trusts May absolutely with his soul and incompletely with his facts. May has earned, repeatedly, the capacity to handle more than the subject credits her with; the subject's underestimation of her resilience is itself a clinical artifact — he cannot fully revise the model of her fragility because the model is welded to the foundational household of his childhood.

**Failure mode.** Any threat to May overrides tactical judgment without exception. The Mephisto bargain is the most extreme documented case — the subject traded the central romantic relationship of his life, against the explicit wishes of his wife, to prevent May's death. This is not strategy; it is the Uncle Ben template firing on automatic. A secondary failure mode is the subject's recurrent inability to ask May for help even when she is positioned and willing to provide it, because the household script forbids burdening her.

**Leverage.** The highest single leverage point in the file. Threat-to-May produces the most rapid degradation of the subject's normal operational discipline of any input identified. Note also that *kindness* to May — an operator who genuinely helps her, befriends her, or provides for her — purchases credit with the subject that he cannot rationally discount even when warranted (the Octavius pattern, late Slott era).

---

## 6.5 RICHARD/MARY PARKER — IMPOSTORS (the false return)

**History.** Discrete operational episode, mid-1990s (Mackie/DeFalco). The subject was approached by figures presenting as his returned biological parents, accepted them initially despite tactical reasons for suspicion, and was subsequently forced to recognize the impersonation.

**Why this deserves its own sub-section.** The episode is the cleanest available diagnostic test of the subject's hunger for parental presence. His operational acuity is high and his baseline skepticism toward improbable returns is well-developed (the clone saga sharpened it considerably). That he nevertheless suspended that skepticism is evidence of an unmet need so large it overrode the file's normal epistemic discipline.

**Failure mode revealed.** The subject's biological-parental hunger is *latent and large*. It is masked in ordinary operation by the May-and-Ben sufficiency, but when activated it is the largest available override of his judgment after the threat-to-May vector.

**Leverage.** Documented and previously successful. The vector remains live.

---

## 6.6 MARY JANE WATSON-PARKER (wife — status continuity-dependent; treated here as origin-bond because she predates the spider-bite in some retellings and is, regardless of marital status, the central adult attachment of the file)

*Full treatment in Relationships II under romantic primaries; included here only to note her structural function as family rather than partner.* MJ is the only person in the subject's life who has occupied all three categories — chosen partner, family, and origin-witness (she identified him as Spider-Man independently in the late Lee/Romita run, *ASM* #42-and-after framing, with the famous disclosure-reveal arriving years later). Her position in the family map is that of the only adult to whom the subject has been able to disclose without precondition. She therefore functions, structurally, as the one node in the file in which the concealment-as-love bind is genuinely escaped. The Mephisto erasure of the marriage is — beneath the cosmological framing — the writers' externalization of the subject's chronic inability to allow himself this escape; the bargain was, psychologically, *over-determined* in a way the in-universe framing does not capture.

---

## 6.7 BENJAMIN "BENJY" PARKER / MAYDAY PARKER and other potential/alternate offspring (the unborn and the elsewhere-born)

**History.** Across mainline canon the subject has no living biological children in continuity at most points of the record. The MC2 alternate continuity (DeFalco) produces May "Mayday" Parker; the late-mainline pregnancy (Clone Saga) is variably resolved across the record as stillbirth, abduction, or erasure; "Renew Your Vows" and other alternates produce surviving children.

**Why this matters even though most of it is non-prime.** The subject's behavioral response to *any* child in a custodial role — Anna Maria Marconi's surrogate-daughter framing during the Superior aftermath, the protective intensity he displays toward teenaged Spider-Man variants (Miles Morales especially), the documented warmth toward May Jameson's namesake — reveals a paternal capacity that has nowhere structural to discharge in mainline continuity. The unborn or elsewhere-born child is a *persistent absence* in the subject's affective economy comparable in kind, though smaller in magnitude, to the parental absence with which his life began.

**Trust/safety.** The subject is unusually safe with children-not-his-own. Civilian children in rescue situations receive a register of voice and physical handling that contrasts markedly with his ordinary adult-facing affect; the quip softens, the verbal register simplifies, the body language opens. This is a stable signature across every artist who has drawn him.

**Failure mode.** Threat to a child in his proximity bypasses tactical caution at a rate approaching the May-threat vector. The Spider-Verse arcs (Slott) demonstrate this with respect to alternate-self children especially.

**Leverage.** Direct: any operation that places a child in observable distress in the subject's presence will redirect his attention and absorb his resources. Indirect: any credible offer of paternity — fulfilled, restored, discovered — would access the same suspension-of-skepticism vector documented in the Richard/Mary impostor episode.

---

## 6.8 THE COMPOSITE PATTERN — what the family map reveals about the subject

The family node-set is structured around three absences (biological father, biological mother, the unborn/alternate child), one foundational dead presence (Ben, the load-bearing column), and one fragile-but-resilient surviving primary (May). The subject's operational personality is built on the proposition that the survivors must be protected at any cost and that the dead must be honored by becoming the kind of person who would not have failed them.

The pattern produces a man who:

- Cannot accept help from family because the family script is bilateral protective concealment;
- Cannot grieve cleanly because each loss layers on top of an unhealed prior loss;
- Cannot disclose without fearing the disclosure will kill or alienate the recipient;
- Cannot rest because rest, in the foundational template, is what permitted the death of the loved one;
- Cannot stop searching for father-figures and cannot reliably distinguish good ones from bad;
- Cannot extinguish the paternal capacity that has no structural outlet in his current life.

The leverage points are, in order of operational severity: threat to May; appearance of a credible paternal-coded recognition figure of intellect; accusation framed in the language of moral omission; threat to any child in his immediate proximity; credible offer of restored biological-parental presence; threat to a romantic primary (treated in Relationships II). Each of these has been exploited at least once in the canonical record. None of them has been resolved.

*— End Section 6. Section 7 (Relationships II — Romantic & Sexual History) to follow.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

*Per UATU directive: each significant non-romantic/non-adversarial attachment in the subject's life, mapped longitudinally. Trust mechanics, failure modes, and the specific way the subject loves each of them — which is never the same way twice.*

---

## I. CHOSEN FAMILY — THE LOAD-BEARING ATTACHMENTS

### 1. May Reilly Parker ("Aunt May")

**Status:** Surviving primary attachment figure. The keystone. Everything else in the subject's emotional architecture rests on her continued breathing.

**History.** Maternal aunt; co-guardian from infancy following the deaths of Richard and Mary Parker. After Ben's murder (AF #15), she becomes the sole surviving original-tier attachment object and acquires, in the subject's psyche, the status of an organ he carries outside his body. Across sixty years of canon the relationship structure has not meaningfully changed: May is the person on whose continued life the subject's stability depends, and to whom he is constitutionally unable to tell the truth in real time. The lie of omission ("she'd worry," "her heart") is rehearsed across every era and every writer.

**Dynamics.** The household dynamic May built — anxious, scarcity-organized, emotionally indirect, food-as-love (the wheatcakes are a clinical detail: a fixed ritual offering that does the affect-work the conversation cannot) — is the subject's template for what care looks like. He replicates it: feeds people, frets about their wellbeing, fails to name his own state. He visits her with measurable frequency (in the Romita and DeFalco eras, every two to three days; in the Slott era, weekly minimum); he calls when he cannot visit; he is incapable of letting more than approximately ninety-six hours pass without confirmation of her status, and his behavior degrades visibly when that interval is forced longer.

**The disclosure event** (JMS, ASM #478/#38, "Happy Birthday" arc) — when May discovers the costume in the laundry and finally confronts him — is the single most important revision of this relationship in canon. Her response (anger, then absorption, then acceptance, then the line *"What did I do wrong, that you couldn't tell me?"*) reorganizes the relationship from concealment-based to acknowledgment-based. The subject's behavior in the months following is measurably different — more direct, less furtive — though the underlying terror of her death is not reduced; if anything it acquires new texture, because she is now also a person who knowingly worries.

**Trust / safety.** Absolute. She is one of perhaps three people on Earth whose presence triggers genuine autonomic downregulation in the subject — the spider-sense quiets, the shoulders drop, the verbal rate slows. Surveillance footage of the subject inside May's kitchen would show a different person than surveillance footage of the subject anywhere else.

**Failure mode.** Catastrophic on two vectors. (a) Her death (or apparent death — ASM #400, DeMatteis — and the later Sins Past / OMD sequences) produces the deepest depressive collapses in the canon; the "One More Day" bargain (JMS, 2007), in which the subject trades his marriage to Mephisto to keep her alive, is the single most diagnostically revealing decision in his adult life — it confirms that May's life is a non-negotiable above every other value he holds, including the relationship he himself has named as the great love of his life. (b) Her *worry* — any evidence that he has caused her distress — produces an immediate, near-childlike collapse into apology and concealment-doubling. She is therefore his greatest stabilizer and his greatest leverage point, and any operator who has identified the bond (the Goblin has, repeatedly) has used it.

**Leverage.** Total. She is, operationally, the kill-switch on the subject's tactical judgment. Any threat to her bypasses cost-benefit analysis entirely.

---

### 2. Benjamin Parker ("Uncle Ben") — *deceased, persistent*

**Status:** Posthumous primary attachment. Functionally still present as internalized voice and moral referent.

**History.** Co-guardian from infancy to subject's adolescence. Killed by Carradine, the burglar the subject deliberately failed to stop (AF #15). The ~48-hour sequence — bite, wrestling cash, the elevator non-intervention, the radio bulletin, the warehouse, the recognition — is the founding trauma and has been re-narrated by virtually every writer who has held the title.

**Dynamics, posthumous.** Ben is the subject's superego, given a name and a face. Internal monologue across runs (DeMatteis, Straczynski especially) shows the subject in continuous, decades-long imagined dialogue with him — at decision points, at failures, in dreams. The "great power / great responsibility" formulation, whatever its actual diegetic origin (retconned several times), functions in the subject's psyche as a directly inherited commandment, the kind of single transmitted sentence that organizes a religious life. The subject re-litigates the night of the murder under stress; sleep-deprivation states bring it close to surface.

**Trust / safety.** The dead Ben is, paradoxically, a regulating presence: imagining what Ben would say is, for the subject, a self-soothing maneuver. The living Ben — what he actually was — is partly mythologized; canonical flashbacks suggest a warm, decent, fallible, financially-pressed man, not the saint the subject's memory has constructed. The mythologization itself is symptomatic: the dead idealized father is easier to fail than the living complicated one.

**Failure mode.** Activated rather than triggered — any narrative or sensory cue that returns the subject to the night of the murder (a similar warehouse, the click of a particular sort of revolver, the phrase "small-time crook") can produce momentary dissociative slippage. The Clone Saga's repeated returns to Ben (Reilly's grave-site monologues, the use of Ben's name as Reilly's surname) operate on this loading.

**Leverage.** Anyone invoking Ben — by name, by image, by analogy — gets through every defense. The Goblin has used this. Kraven understood it implicitly.

---

### 3. Mary Jane Watson-Parker

*Mapped fully in Relationships I (Intimate / Romantic). Cross-referenced here as the chosen-family axis: she is, functionally, his second home, and the only person besides May whose presence produces autonomic downregulation. The trust architecture is romantic but the attachment substrate is family-of-choice; she is the only adult relationship in which he has sustained disclosure without rupture.*

---

### 4. Anna Maria Marconi

**Status:** Post-Superior chosen-family / surrogate-daughter figure. One of the most unusual and clinically revealing attachments in the file.

**History.** Met the subject's body during the Superior arc, while Octavius was occupying it; she fell in love with that occupant, was nearly married to him, and was abandoned mid-engagement when Octavius vacated. The subject, upon repossession of his body, inherits the relationship as wreckage — a woman who loved a man who was not him, who knew his body in ways he does not remember, and who is owed an explanation he cannot fully give without exposing the entire metaphysics.

**Dynamics.** What emerges, across Slott's Spider-Verse and Renew Your Vows-adjacent runs, is one of the most adult relationships in his canon. He does not try to make her love him; he tries to make her *safe* and *seen*. He brings her into Parker Industries as Chief of Staff, defers to her engineering judgment (she is one of the very few people whose technical work he treats as a peer's rather than a student's), and protects her at a remove. The relationship reads as protective-fraternal-with-guilt-component; she is, in effect, a member of his household whom he failed before he met her and is now attempting to repay across years.

**Trust / safety.** High operational trust, lower emotional trust — he tells her *what* he is doing, less often *how he feels*. She is one of the few people allowed inside the lab without performance.

**Failure mode.** Subject's guilt-debt to her runs hot; any reminder of the Superior episode (her own confusion about her own past, a returning memory, an Octavius-shaped echo) destabilizes him more than her. He will sabotage the relationship rather than allow her to feel re-victimized — which means he sometimes withholds the very contact she would benefit from.

**Leverage.** Moderate. Threats to her activate the protective protocol but do not bypass tactical judgment the way May/MJ threats do; she is family-tier but not keystone-tier.

---

### 5. Mayday Parker / "May" / surrogate-daughter figures across continuities

**Status:** Symbolic / aspirational. Across alternates (MC2, Renew Your Vows, Spider-Verse arcs), the subject is repeatedly written as a father, and these depictions are diagnostic even where non-canonical, because writers consistently identify fatherhood as the *resolution* of his structure rather than as a new arena of failure.

**Dynamics.** Where given a daughter, the subject becomes more, not less, regulated. The protective frame is the same — he would die for her instantly — but the rumination quiets. The Uncle Ben loop appears to partially close when he is on the giving end of the transmission rather than the receiving end. This is clinically coherent: an internalized loss organized around inherited responsibility partially resolves when the responsibility is discharged to a successor.

**Leverage.** Maximal in any continuity where the daughter exists. The subject's protective threshold for a daughter-figure is below even May's.

---

## II. THE INNER CIRCLE — PEERS WHO KNOW

### 6. Johnny Storm (the Human Torch)

**Status:** Best friend (peer-tier). The closest non-romantic, non-familial bond in the canon. Has named himself such repeatedly; the subject reciprocates with characteristic obliquity.

**History.** Met early (Strange Tales Annual #2, ASM #1) in mutual antagonism; the relationship matured across the Lee/Romita era through repeated team-ups; codified as best-friendship across the DeFalco, Wieringo, Slott, and Spencer eras; most fully realized in the post-Johnny-death membership in the Future Foundation (Hickman, FF #1 onward), when the subject took Johnny's seat at the Richards table — an act of grief-substitution that doubled as a public declaration of brotherhood.

**Dynamics.** The single relationship in which the subject's quipping is not a defense. With Johnny, the humor is play — call-and-response, mutual pranking, the *only* sustained recreational verbal activity in the entire file. The window-prank tradition (Spider-Man and the Torch leaving each other elaborate booby-trapped surprises) is the closest the subject comes to depicted leisure. Johnny is the one peer with whom the subject is not performing competence; he is permitted to be wrong, silly, undignified.

**Trust / safety.** High and asymmetric in an interesting direction: Johnny knows the secret, has met May, has been to the apartment, has crashed on the couch. He is permitted into the domestic sphere in a way almost no other ally is. But Johnny does *not* know the depressive interior — the subject keeps the catastrophic affect away from him, because Johnny is the one room in the house where joy is permitted, and the subject will not contaminate it.

**Failure mode.** Johnny's death (Hickman) produced one of the cleanest grief-presentations in the subject's canon — visible, articulated, partially integrated. Notable because it was a grief the subject was *allowed* to have (no guilt attachment — he wasn't there, he didn't cause it), and the cleanness of the grief by contrast illuminates how fundamentally guilt-distorted every other grief he carries is.

**Leverage.** Real but not catastrophic — Johnny is durable enough, and peer-tier enough, that the subject does not feel uniquely responsible for him. A rare and healthy feature.

---

### 7. Matt Murdock (Daredevil)

**Status:** Brother-in-arms. The peer who shares the operational reality most precisely — masked, neighborhood-tier, religiously guilt-organized, Catholic-flavored where the subject's guilt is secular-Jewish-coded (per various writer leanings; the underlying structure is similar).

**History.** Crossed paths from early Lee era; the relationship deepened across Miller's Daredevil run and the Bendis/Maleev era; codified as mutual-disclosure brotherhood when Murdock revealed himself (post-Frank Miller) and the subject reciprocated with cape-tier disclosure.

**Dynamics.** Of all the subject's allies, Murdock is the one who can *hear* the quip's function in real time — Murdock's literal auditory acuity registers the heart-rate spike under the joke, the catch in the breath, the subvocal swallow. The subject knows this. Around Murdock, the quipping decreases by a measurable margin; the subject talks less because talking accomplishes less. The relationship runs on shared diagnosis: each of them sees the other's pathology clearly, neither requires the other to fix it.

**Trust / safety.** High operational, high informational, moderate emotional — they will tell each other tactical truth instantly and personal truth slowly. Murdock has been to the apartment; he has met MJ; he has on at least one occasion (Bendis) protected the subject's identity at significant legal cost.

**Failure mode.** Murdock's own catastrophes (the *Born Again* arc, the Shadowland period, the Kingpin-mayor arc) put the subject in the position of helper rather than helped; the subject's ability to *receive* care from Murdock is limited by the same pattern that limits his receipt of care from anyone. He sends help and accepts none.

**Leverage.** Moderate — threats to Murdock activate the protective protocol but the subject knows Murdock is competent. The greater leverage is *moral*: Murdock's disapproval lands harder than almost any other peer's, because Murdock's moral judgment shares the subject's own structure.

---

### 8. Felicia Hardy (the Black Cat)

*Mapped primarily in Relationships I. Cross-referenced here because in the post-Slott era she has been written as an unstable ally / chosen-family-edge figure as much as a romantic axis. Trust is conditional and rupture-prone; the subject loves her with a permanent reservation that she correctly perceives.*

---

### 9. Robbie Robertson

**Status:** The non-pathological paternal-mentor figure in the subject's professional life. The healthy counterweight to Jameson.

**History.** City editor at the *Daily Bugle* throughout the Lee/Romita and Stern eras; sustained presence across decades; the only Bugle figure who consistently believed in the subject (both as photographer and, intuitively, as person), who recognized something was going on without demanding to know what, and who shielded him from Jameson's volatility at quiet cost to himself.

**Dynamics.** Robertson modeled, for the subject, what an honorable adult professional looks like — quietly principled, family-rooted (the Randy Robertson subplots), willing to take a personal hit (his eventual imprisonment over the Tombstone arc, JMS-adjacent) for the truth. The subject's own later attempts at professional integrity have Robbie's fingerprints on them.

**Trust / safety.** High and one-directional — the subject trusts Robertson far more than Robertson knows, and would do almost anything for him on quiet request. Robertson is one of the few non-cape figures the subject has consulted at decision points.

**Failure mode.** Robertson's vulnerabilities (the Tombstone leverage, the imprisonment) produced uncharacteristic rages in the subject — quiet, slow-burn, sustained, rather than reactive. He does not lose Robbie loudly.

**Leverage.** Substantial — Robertson is one of perhaps a dozen people whose harm the subject would treat as personal. Unknown to most operators.

---

### 10. Ben Urich

**Status:** Trusted civilian-press ally. Held the subject's secret-adjacent material for years without exploitation.

**Dynamics.** Urich is the kind of person the subject respects most: a competent professional doing necessary work at personal cost. The relationship is professional-warm, low-frequency, high-trust on the narrow band of journalistic ethics. Urich's instinct to *not* publish certain truths because of their human cost is something the subject has, in private monologue, identified as a kind of heroism parallel to his own.

**Trust / safety.** High informational, low emotional. They are not friends in the kitchen-coffee sense; they are allies in the trench sense.

---

## III. THE TEAM-TIER ALLIES — AVENGERS / FANTASTIC FOUR / X-ADJACENT

### 11. Reed and Sue Richards (the Fantastic Four collective)

**Status:** Quasi-family. The Baxter Building has been, intermittently, a second domestic space for the subject — most explicitly during the Future Foundation period when he held Johnny's seat at meals.

**Dynamics.** With Reed, the subject is one of perhaps four people on Earth who can keep up in the lab; the relationship is collegial-respectful, with the subject playing slightly-deferential younger genius to Reed's senior genius, though across the Slott/Spencer eras he is increasingly written as a peer. With Sue, the relationship is maternal-warm and protective in both directions; Sue is one of the very few women the subject is unproblematically comfortable around — neither romantic charge nor surrogate-mother projection, just adult care. He calls her "Sue," not "Mrs. Richards," and she has fed him at least as often as he has fed himself in the Future Foundation period.

**Trust / safety.** High, family-tier without the catastrophic-leverage loading; he is permitted in their home, with their children, unsupervised.

**Failure mode.** When the Richardses fail morally (Reed in *Civil War*, the cloning of Thor, the secret cabal), the subject's disappointment is severe and slow — he does not rage at Reed, he withdraws, and the withdrawal lasts.

---

### 12. Tony Stark

**Status:** Complicated mentor / surrogate-father figure. The relationship the subject's behavior most clearly demonstrates he both wanted and could not safely have.

**History.** Distant ally across most of the canon; brought into close orbit during the New Avengers (Bendis) and especially *Civil War* (Millar), when Stark mentored the subject as the public face of the Registration Act, designed the Iron Spider suit, brought him into Avengers Tower with MJ and May, and *encouraged the public unmasking* — the single most catastrophic operational decision in the subject's adult life.

**Dynamics.** The subject was hungry for Stark's mentorship in a way that, in retrospect, illuminates the size of the father-shaped vacancy in his life. He accepted gifts (the suit), accepted housing, accepted strategic counsel — and then, when Stark's moral compromise became undeniable (the Negative Zone prison, the cloning of Thor, the persecution of Cap), the subject's defiance was proportional to the prior trust: total, public, and costly. The post-*Civil War* break is one of the cleanest "kill the father" sequences in the canon.

**Trust / safety.** Was high; was destroyed; has been partially rebuilt (post-Slott, post-Spencer) at significantly reduced amplitude. The subject will never again accept that depth of patronage from Stark.

**Failure mode.** Stark is the proof-of-concept that the subject *cannot* accept father-figures in their full role without eventual catastrophe — because his standard for fathers is Ben, and Ben is dead and mythologized, and no living man can sustain the comparison without eventually being seen to fail it.

**Leverage.** Now low. Once high.

---

### 13. Steve Rogers (Captain America)

**Status:** Moral north-star. The peer-mentor whose authority the subject accepts because it is *earned through suffering visibly equivalent to his own*.

**Dynamics.** With Rogers, the subject behaves better than he behaves anywhere else — straighter back, fewer jokes (though the jokes still appear, and Rogers, to his credit, lets them land), more direct speech. Rogers is the one authority figure the subject defers to without the oscillation-into-defiance pattern that governs Stark, Fury, and Jameson; this is because Rogers' moral structure passes the subject's deeply suspicious vetting. The famous *Civil War* defection — the subject switching to Cap's side at significant personal cost — is the behavioral demonstration of where the trust gradient sits.

**Trust / safety.** High, asymmetric — the subject trusts Rogers more than Rogers reciprocally trusts him, because Rogers does not fully see the depressive interior, and the subject is too embarrassed to display it before a man he reads as cleaner than himself.

**Failure mode.** If Rogers were ever to morally collapse (the Hydra-Cap arc, *Secret Empire*), the subject's destabilization would be severe — and was, when it briefly happened.

**Leverage.** Moral, not operational.

---

### 14. Nick Fury (the elder)

**Status:** Authority-figure with whom the relationship is permanently transactional and permanently mistrustful.

**Dynamics.** Fury is the closest figure in the subject's life to his actual dead parents — same world, same operational logic, same ethical permissibility-zone. The subject's deep, ambient discomfort around Fury is, in part, displaced material from the unprocessed knowledge (acquired across various retcons, especially the *Trust* arc) that his parents were Fury-type assets and that Fury knew them. He cooperates with Fury when interests align; he refuses Fury when they don't; he never, ever takes Fury at his word.

**Trust / safety.** Low informational, moderate operational — they will fight together; they will not share.

---

### 15. Jessica Drew (Spider-Woman) and Carol Danvers

**Status:** Peer-tier Avengers-era allies; emotionally functional sister-coded friendships.

**Dynamics with Drew.** The shared arachnid-coded operational territory, combined with Drew's own catastrophic biographical loading (the Skrull replacement, the resulting identity-instability), gives the subject and Drew a shorthand that operates below verbal level. He can be tired around her. She does not require him to perform.

**Dynamics with Danvers.** More formal, more military-coded; the subject respects her command authority and follows it in team contexts, though the underlying defiance-trigger is present if she crosses into Stark-zone moral compromise (as during *Civil War II*, where the subject sided against her).

---

### 16. Wolverine / Logan

**Status:** Unlikely brother. One of the canon's most clinically interesting friendships.

**Dynamics.** Logan, on paper, is everything the subject's moral structure should reject — the kill-rule violator, the man who has done everything the subject has structured his life around not doing. And yet across the New Avengers, Astonishing, and various Slott runs, the relationship functions as a quietly stabilizing mutual-recognition: each of them carries unbearable guilt loads, each metabolizes them differently, each respects the other's chosen metabolism. Logan, notably, *does not mock the quip* — he registers it, leaves it alone, talks under it to the actual man. He is one of very few who do.

**Trust / safety.**


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*UATU dossier, Section 7-C. Subject: PARKER, P. B. Cross-reference Sections 7-A (Family) and 7-B (Allies & Civilian Network). All entries derived from longitudinal behavioral observation across the published canon; clinical anchors per Chapman (2019), DOI: 10.1017/S0954579419000658.*

A note before the granular entries. The subject's enmities and his intimacies are not, structurally, separate categories. Both involve a person who has been allowed past the perimeter — the perimeter being the line at which the quip stops working. Strangers get the quip. Lovers and archenemies get the silence behind it. This is why his villains so reliably know things about him that his coworkers do not, and why his romantic ruptures so often read like supervillain origin stories. What follows maps both arcs because they share a single underlying mechanism: the subject's compulsive need to be *fully seen* — even (especially) by people who will use the seeing against him.

---

## I. RIVALS & ENEMIES

### Norman Osborn — the Green Goblin

**History.** Original confrontation, ASM #14 (1964); identity unmasked, ASM #39–40; the death of Gwen Stacy at his hands, ASM #121; his apparent death in ASM #122 and his resurrection across the 1996 Osborn reveal; "Sins Past" (JMS); the Dark Reign / Osborn-as-public-hero period; the "Goblin Nation" finale of the Slott Superior arc; recurrences through Spencer and Wells.

**Dynamics.** This is the central enemy and the only one whose existence the subject organizes around in the same register he organizes around Ben Parker. Osborn is, functionally, the negative father — the wealthy, brilliant, capable patriarch (Harry's father, not his; the inverse of Ben in every variable except authority) who chose to kill the woman the subject loved and who has, ever since, made killing the people the subject loves into a recurring discipline. The dynamic is not adversarial in the ordinary sense; it is *intimate*. Osborn studies him. Osborn knows the secret identity longer and more thoroughly than any other adversary. Osborn collects the names. Osborn weaponizes the family.

**Evolution.** First era (pre-Gwen): a high-end rogue, dangerous but containable. Gwen's death recategorizes him permanently — he becomes the only figure against whom the no-kill rule actively trembles. The post-Gwen beating in ASM #122 is the single most uncontrolled physical episode in the subject's recorded history; the writer's pencil notes (Romita, Conway interviews) describe Peter swinging not to subdue but to *destroy*, and only the glider impalement removes the question of whether he would have stopped. Osborn's later resurrections force the subject to live with the fact that the man who killed Gwen *is not dead and he, Peter, did not kill him* — which is structurally a relief and a fresh wound at once. Across Dark Reign, Osborn weaponizes legitimacy itself, occupying the institutional positions Peter is locked out of by Parker Luck; the asymmetry (Osborn rich, public, sanctioned; Peter broke, anonymous, hunted) is the exact inverse the subject was raised inside, and his rage at Osborn always contains a thread of class-grievance the writers occasionally surface.

**Trust/Safety.** Zero. The subject does not believe Osborn can be rehabilitated, does not believe in his "cured" periods (correctly), and treats every reported death as provisional. This is the only relationship in which the subject's pessimism is fully calibrated to the evidence.

**Failure mode.** Tactical degradation under Osborn-presence. The subject becomes louder, more reckless, more willing to take hits he would otherwise slip; the quip becomes serrated. Around no other adversary does he allow himself to want to *hurt*. This is the closest the subject comes to revealing the structure beneath the structure.

**Leverage.** Osborn holds, at various points: Peter's identity, Aunt May's location, MJ's location, the Stacy secret, and — most damagingly — the suggestion (real or fabricated) that Gwen bore his children. The "Sins Past" arc is interpretively contested in-universe but the *behavioral* outcome is unambiguous: Osborn weaponized the unprocessed grief itself, and the subject's response was prolonged depressive withdrawal. Osborn's leverage is not informational; it is *interpretive*. He gets to keep editing the meaning of the worst day of Peter's life.

---

### Otto Octavius — Doctor Octopus

**History.** First confrontation, ASM #3 (1963); the Master Planner arc, ASM #31–33; near-marriage to Aunt May, ASM #131; the long roster of recurrences; the body-theft (*Superior Spider-Man*, 2012–14); the post-Superior coexistence and reluctant collaboration through Slott and Spencer.

**Dynamics.** The mirror enemy. Octavius is what the subject would have been had Ben Parker been a different man, had peer rejection produced bitterness rather than humor, had the intellect not been disciplined by guilt. They share: orphan-coded biographies, hyper-intellectualism, mechanical-spatial genius, and a body permanently fused to a piece of accident-derived hardware. Their fights are the only fights in which the subject is genuinely outclassed *cognitively* — every other rogue Peter outthinks; Octavius is the one who, on a bad day, outthinks him.
